<div id="review_955423140" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/955423140" />
    <a title="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans"><img alt="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587115780p2/7298986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/955423140?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" href="/user/show/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans">✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/38643148-perdido-street-station">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=blah-blah-blah-heaven">blah-blah-blah-heaven</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=boredom-fest">boredom-fest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=welcome-to-the-zoo">welcome-to-the-zoo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=tbr-shelf-says-thank-you">tbr-shelf-says-thank-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=cure-for-insomnia">cure-for-insomnia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=outrageously-overhyped-fishsauce">outrageously-overhyped-fishsauce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=dnf-graveyard">dnf-graveyard</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=machalo-tbr-cleaning-challenge-2018">machalo-tbr-cleaning-challenge-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer955423140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4239018997164329606"><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1423050384ra/13581493.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>☠ <b>DNF at 11%</b>. <i>Go me and stuff.</i><br/><br/><b>Yet another</b> <s>overhyped</s> book with a cult following bites the dust! Yay! <b>I obviously read this one wrong</b>! Or maybe I read it right but didn't enjoy it because I'd mistakenly purchased the <b>Swahili version</b> and read it <b>back to front and upside down</b>. This is the most plausible explanation, since I don't belong to the <b>People of Despicable Book Taste Horde</b> (PoDBTH™) and always read books <b>right</b>. <br/><br/><b>Had I bought the English version</b>, I'm pretty sure I wouldn't have noticed how exc</span>
  <span id="freeText4239018997164329606" style="display:none"><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1423050384ra/13581493.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>☠ <b>DNF at 11%</b>. <i>Go me and stuff.</i><br><br><b>Yet another</b> <s>overhyped</s> book with a cult following bites the dust! Yay! <b>I obviously read this one wrong</b>! Or maybe I read it right but didn't enjoy it because I'd mistakenly purchased the <b>Swahili version</b> and read it <b>back to front and upside down</b>. This is the most plausible explanation, since I don't belong to the <b>People of Despicable Book Taste Horde</b> (PoDBTH™) and always read books <b>right</b>. <br><br><b>Had I bought the English version</b>, I'm pretty sure I wouldn't have noticed how excruciatingly <b>boring</b> the story is. Or thought that reading the book was a more <b>tedious chore</b> than being on cleaning duty at the barnacle shed. But I didn't, so I did. Bloody stinking fish, rarely have I had to suffer through such <b>painfully overdescriptive prose</b>. And I thought <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/127455.The_Lies_of_Locke_Lamora__Gentleman_Bastard___1_" title="The Lies of Locke Lamora (Gentleman Bastard, #1) by Scott Lynch" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lies of Locke Lamora</a> was bad! Silly little shrimp that I am. Locke Lamora is a <b>complete joke</b> compared to this painfully harrowing effort, like reading one of <b>Noddy's fascinating adventures</b> or something. Anyway, I have to admit this wasn't <b>completely unexpected</b>. I mean, I was going to read the ebook version of this most beguiling tale but a friend (whom I shall be eternally full of grate to) mentioned the book was awfully, um, generous, in the <b>details department</b>, and recommended I listened to the audio version to <s>ease the pain</s> enjoy the book <b>to its full potential</b>. That was one of the mostest brilliantest ideas ever, I have to say. I'm pretty sure I wouldn't have made it <b>past the prologue</b>, had I read the printed version. Okay, so I didn't make it past the 11% mark, which isn't much better, but that's <b>completely irrelevant</b> right now. Of course it is. Anyway, the cool thing about the audio for this book is that, even if you <b>forget to hit the pause button</b> while you're busy <s>slaughtering puny humans</s> running errands, you don't miss a <b>bloody shrimping thing</b>. Because then you come back and realize the narrator is <b>still babbling about the same stuff</b> he was <b>rambling about</b> half an hour before! <b>How cool is that</b>?!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1519726519ra/25130024.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Talking about the lovely narrator</b>. I'm pretty sure Mr John Lee is a positively delightful human being, but never in my crustacean life have I listened to a more <b>pompous, overly theatrical narration</b>. It sounded like the guy was chanting ancient poetry, for fish's sake! Okay, in his defense, Miéville's <b>exceedingly convoluted and needlessly wordy writing</b> probably didn't help his performance. Quite the <b>Perniciously Deadly Combination</b> (PDC™) these two make. So. Long story short: I got to chapter 8 and started feeling <b>a teensy little bit like</b>…<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1519727333ra/25130074.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>My murderous troops</b> obviously started getting very concerned about my <b>mental health</b>, so I decided to hit the pause button one gloriously final time, and proceeded to DNF the fish out of this most wondrous piece of literature. <b>Yes, I did it for the kids' sake</b>. I hate to see them distressed, you see. Had it not been for them, I would gladly have continued listening to this delightful story. Obviously. Right. Absolutely. Indeed. <b>No doubt about that</b> and stuff. Okay, so to be disgustingly honest, it kinda sorta sucks big time that I was forced to DNF this book against my nefarious will. Because, to be revoltingly candid, the world Miéville created here is pretty fishing <b>amazing and creative and original and unique and beautifully dark and stuff</b>. Also, the very diverse cast of <b>characters and creatures</b> is pretty shrimping awesome. Especially Lin the <b>insectoid artist</b>. Yeah, she's sort of cool for a, um, you know, <b>bug</b>. And I'm kind of an expert on the topic. I mean, I survived the *shudders pre-emptively* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15937790-endgame" rel="nofollow noopener">Sirantha Jax Debacle of Doom and Oblivion and Epic Proportions</a> (SJDoDaOaEP™), so I know a lot about hexapod invertebrates in books, and how they can <b>viciously and most thoroughly ruin</b> potentially stupendous series and stuff. Damn, I'm convulsing just thinking about it. Better change the subject before my allergic reaction comes back in full swing, and my exoskeleton starts getting all <b>swollen and blotchy and stuff</b>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1518795140i/25071684.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>I'm trying! I'm trying! But this Sirantha Jax business was ever so slightly traumatizing and stuff, so I might need a few gallons of whisky to numb the somewhat excruciating pain and calm the fish down.</i><br><br><b>The End</b>.<br><br>➽ And the moral of this <b>Surprise Surprise I Just DNFed a Book with a Stellar Average Rating and Glowing Reviews Now That is Most Uncommon Indeed Crappy Non Review</b> is (SSIJDaBwaSARaGRNTiMUICNR™): this story could have been <b>wrondrously wondrous</b>. Only that it wasn't. <b>Oh well</b>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1428389872i/14414736.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479540926i/21203689._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>Pre-review nonsense</b><br><br><b>Listening</b> to 2.42 hours of this book felt like losing <b>30 years of my life</b>. Patting my little self on the exoskeleton for <b>DNFing the fish out of it</b>. I mean, had that not been the case, I'm pretty sure I would have <b>gone extinct</b> before getting to the 50% mark. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491150488i/22380629.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>➽ Full <b>Phew Bloody Stinking Fish That Was Close This One Nearly Did Me In and Stuff Crappy Non Review</b> (PBSFTWCTONDMIaSCNR™) to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4239018997164329606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2702454534" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2702454534" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2702454534?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2702454534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6243009145992941022">While China Mieville’s Perdido Street Station sometimes takes wide detours around the plot, the atmosphere he conjures, with its dark and sinister underpinnings, is interesting and compelling. There are parts of the book that make you smell what’s happening in this world. Let me say this off the bat: it’s not a good smell! Even the way Mieville demonstrates the artistic method (in the form of MC’s insect girlfriend) is rather unique. And while it doesn’t advance the plot, it had me thinking abou</span>
  <span id="freeText6243009145992941022" style="display:none">While China Mieville’s Perdido Street Station sometimes takes wide detours around the plot, the atmosphere he conjures, with its dark and sinister underpinnings, is interesting and compelling. There are parts of the book that make you smell what’s happening in this world. Let me say this off the bat: it’s not a good smell! Even the way Mieville demonstrates the artistic method (in the form of MC’s insect girlfriend) is rather unique. And while it doesn’t advance the plot, it had me thinking about the creative process. The prose is quite different from The City &amp; The City; however, there’s something about the way Mieveille creates worlds which connects the books and I think challenges readers to become immersed in strange and alien worlds. The novel is dense (crammed full of ideas and concepts), but it is still accessible. I plan to read more of this series, but I’ll probably reread Perdido Street Station at least one more time first. 4.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6243009145992941022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1963609850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1963609850" />
    <a title="Stephan " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48197248-stephan"><img alt="Stephan " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529844140p2/48197248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1963609850?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephan " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephan " href="/user/show/48197248-stephan">Stephan </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1963609850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16230370850853813697"><b>Finished. I am stunned. 5-star stunned.</b> After having read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___the_City' title='The City & the City by China Miéville' rel='nofollow noopener'>The City & the City</a> I new I was in for something special, but I had no idea is was going to be anything like this. Perdido Street Station is a rich steampunk fantasy novel. The world is unique and filled to the brim with creative ideas and details. Every sense is involved when wandering through it. <br/><br/><b>If you want to read this, don't be faint of heart.</b> The visuals are sometimes shocking and early on there are animal experiments, then - no spo</span>
  <span id="freeText16230370850853813697" style="display:none"><b>Finished. I am stunned. 5-star stunned.</b> After having read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___the_City" title="The City &amp; the City by China Miéville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City &amp; the City</a> I new I was in for something special, but I had no idea is was going to be anything like this. Perdido Street Station is a rich steampunk fantasy novel. The world is unique and filled to the brim with creative ideas and details. Every sense is involved when wandering through it. <br /><br /><b>If you want to read this, don't be faint of heart.</b> The visuals are sometimes shocking and early on there are animal experiments, then - no spoiling - things happen to corpses and boy, do they stink. There are beasts of hell and sewers - no, not the harmless kind, these are filthy with slick walls, slime, mould, full of rot and shit. If you are prone to nightmares, here you are supplied with lots of material. Is it horror? No it is not, but there sure are elements. <br /><br />Mieville's vivid language gives birth to the city of New Crobuzon. It grows in your mind, takes on form. The inhabitants, the smell, the looks.. the buildings..the city burst forth and spreads out before your eyes. His choice of words and his vocabulary is monumentous.. <br /><br /><i>"Termagant!" he moaned after her. "Shrew! Harridan! All right, all right, you win, you, you... uh... virago, you spit-fire..."</i><br /><br />There is no doubt, I like the main character. He and his friends have morals but they are challenged. Is there a good way to do something bad? Can crimes be redeemed? Many questions are posed in this book. That's part of what I like so much about Mieville's books. While telling a story he asks questions and reflects on them, indirectly. No need to deal with them if you don't want to, but if you do, you are challenged.<br /><br />There was something I didn't like. At first I was surprised, but then I realized he did this in The City &amp; The City too. Often there is this switch in the telling of a story near the end of a book, that I dislike. It occurs at the precise moment when the main character after struggling suddenly finds clarity and finally has a plan. This plan is not shared for the purpose of suspence. From this point on, the reader isn't part of the story anymore, (s)he is merely permitted to watch the plan unfold. I'm not saying this is a flaw or that this technique to build suspense doesn't work - it's just that I feel the moment happening and I feel shut out. But luckily it doesn't make the book any worse.<br /><br /><b>Now go, get the book, read your heart out. Open your mind and let Mieville draw you in, astound you, shock you, let him paint on the canvas of your imagination .. but hold tight, you are in for a real and captivating ride!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="16230370850853813697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_58173627" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58173627" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58173627?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=scifi-future-speculative-fict">scifi-future-speculative-fict</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58173627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11316454055771436825">I've read three other Mievilles before this, and they were <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72921519' rel='nofollow noopener'>2*</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396185571' rel='nofollow noopener'>4*</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396183492' rel='nofollow noopener'>5*</a>.<br/><br/>I'm so pleased this was another 5*. What a wonderful, rich, steampunky, fantastical phantasmagoria this is.<br/><br/><b>PLOT</b><br/><br/>It opens with one of several short, first-person impressions: a newcomer arriving by boat at night. He’s wealthy but anguished, and the boatman fears him. <br/><br/>The story then opens in New Crobuzon: an ancient city (some houses nearly 1000 years old) inhabited by many exotic sentient species. We meet Lin, a khepri (inse</span>
  <span id="freeText11316454055771436825" style="display:none">I've read three other Mievilles before this, and they were <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72921519" rel="nofollow noopener">2*</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396185571" rel="nofollow noopener">4*</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396183492" rel="nofollow noopener">5*</a>.<br /><br />I'm so pleased this was another 5*. What a wonderful, rich, steampunky, fantastical phantasmagoria this is.<br /><br /><b>PLOT</b><br /><br />It opens with one of several short, first-person impressions: a newcomer arriving by boat at night. He’s wealthy but anguished, and the boatman fears him. <br /><br />The story then opens in New Crobuzon: an ancient city (some houses nearly 1000 years old) inhabited by many exotic sentient species. We meet Lin, a khepri (insect) artist, and her boyfriend, Isaac, a maverick human scientist. Not everyone approves of inter-species relationships, and Isaac seems to relish the semi-secrecy: "’My monster. I am a pervert’ thought Isaac, ‘and so is she’”. But there is always ambivalence; when she turns up unexpectedly, he “felt anger and affection jostle”. <br /><br />Both take on dodgy commissions. Isaac is asked to help Yagharek fly. He’s a bird-like garuda who had his wings removed for “choice-theft”. Meanwhile, Lin agrees to make a sculpture of Mr Motley, an underworld figure who is multiply Remade (hybrid of various species).<br /><br />Then a beautiful, totally original, but terrifying new sort of creature is loose in the city and strange allegiances are made to try to find a solution.<br /><br />There is a huge cast, and a complex plot, but it’s never confusing, and there is real and profound development of all the main characters. The first part is fairly leisurely, introducing the characters and their world; the later sections are fast and furious.<br /><br /><b>TRANSITION, DIFFERENCE, CLEVAGE, BOUNDARIES</b><br /><br />“I believe this to be the fundamental dynamic. Transition. The point where one thing becomes another.” Mr Motley is ostensibly talking about art, daring Lin to cross her own boundaries to experience something new, even if it ends up so individualized that she can't convey it to an audience, but it applies to everything in this story.<br /><br />When Isaac tries to find a way to power Yag’s flight, he considers watercraft, unified field theory, Torque energy and crisis energy, all of which are about things on the cusp of transition.<br /><br />Many of the characters have transitions in their own bodies: some are Remade, some metamorphose, others have physiognomies with unusual boundaries. For instance, cactus people have “a moment when the skin… of the sentient creature, becomes mindless plant.”<br /><br /><b>SPECIESISM and FEMINISM</b><br /><br />Although there is a degree of segregation by species and general prejudice against some of them, any analogy with racism, let alone Apartheid, is incidental, though the freak-show makes for uncomfortable reading. <br /><br />Isaac has mixed feelings at his first sexual encounter with Lin, “for just a moment after coming, Isaac had been overcome with revulsion… When he had woken he had felt fearful and horrified, but at the fact of having transgressed rather than at the transgression itself” and “after the atavistic disgust and fear had gone, leaving only a nervous, very deep affection”.<br /><br />Mieville’s feminist credentials are evident. There are plenty of strong female characters (including a mercenary) and when women are treated badly, it’s clearly condemned. There is also a significant passage where one character faces the reality of prostitution, after which he foreswears it. <br /><br />The slake-moths are hermaphrodite, but it’s the STRONGEST one who has the right to be mother (the others are “accepting defeat and masculinity”). Such gender fluidity also fits with the transition theme. <br /><br /><b>FOOD</b><br /><br />The first chapter opens with Lin getting food, and by buying meat, she gives away the fact that Isaac is there. Amongst multicultural types, like Lin's friends, the main feature of mixed-species relationships seems to be the relatively trivial aspect of food, but food, in the broadest sense, is a recurring theme: <br /><br />•	Dietary differences are a visceral issue for Isaac: "As he watched her [eat], Isaac felt the familiar trilling of emotion: disgust immediately stamped out, pride at the stamping out, guilty desire." <br />•	Lin’s gland-art has an inherently digestive dimension - even more so when she adds colourberries to her spit. <br />•	For no obvious reason, the slaughterhouse process of slitting throats and letting blood drain away is described. It’s oddly akin to halal or kosher - except that these are pigs! <br />•	Isaac tests his crisis engine on cheese.<br />•	The whole population becomes a food source for something that has no natural predators: “The very air was thick with dreams, and the flying things lapped eagerly at the succulent juices.”<br />•	The Weaver can “subsist on the appreciation of beauty”. <br />•	The Construct Council says “my sustenance is information”. <br />•	And drugs are consumed and do consume...<br />	<br /><b>COMMUNALITY and CONSCIOUSNESS</b><br /><br />This book doesn’t promote communal living, let alone communism, but there are varied examples of group living and thought. <br /><br />Lin works alone, but most khepri art is a communal effort, as is their society generally (including sex). Garuda society is also communal and egalitarian, which is why choice-theft is so bad.<br /><br />There are two, very different, sorts of hive mind: one organic and one not: "We too became I", which is equivalent to “we two (or more) became one”.<br /><br />This raises lots of questions about the nature of consciousness, both for various types of hive minds, but also for non-organic intelligence. <br /><br />The Construct Council is like cross between the internet and a supercomputer “born of random power and virus and chance”, it became a “self-creating god… My computational power is unprecedented… my sustenance is information… I do not dream... I am a calculating machine that has calculated how to think." And perhaps, most chillingly, "I compute, so I am." However, it is not entirely mechanical: “The control that the Council wielded over his human followers disturbed her… she could not understand what release or service this heretical church offered its congregation".<br /><br /><b>INSECT SEX</b><br /><br />The (brief) descriptions of Isaac and Lin’s sex life are startling, but it’s the descriptions of Lin’s childhood experiences that are troubling.<br /><br />She was raised in the Insect Aspect belief system, with females being inherently fallen and thus adoring of and servile to the males (sadly familiar in relation to OT-based Christianity), but the communal incestual sex abuse, at the behest of her broodma was awful. "Lin remembered coming home to a house that swarmed with male khepri... She remembered being commanded to wash her innumerable brothers' glistening carapaces... to let them scuttle over her and explore her body as their dumb curiosity directed them." “Headsex for procreation was an unpleasant chore carried out for demographic duty”. In contrast, most recreational sex is between females, “communal… but rather ritualised”. "Her friend introduced her to pleasuresex... Her body had been a source of shame and disgust... Until then she had been subjected only to headsex at her mother's behest, sitting still and uncomfortable while a male scrabbled and coupled excitedly with her headscarab, in mercifully unsuccessful attempts at procreation." <br /><br />Lin now lives where that's not the norm, but she is still trying to come to terms with her past.<br /><br /><b>VISIONS</b><br /><br />There is a plague of nightmares, as well as hallucinations, drug trips, synaesthesia, and multi-dimensional beings. How do we know what is real and true?<br /><br />“Dreams were becoming a pestilence… They even inveigled their way into the minds of the waking… All over the city the night was fissured by cries of nocturnal misery.” <br /><br />Hypnotic beauty can be terrifying, “Wings – of unstable dimensions and shapes, beating as they do in various planes”<br /><br />There are even different types of vision/sight. Lin tries to explain what it's like to see the world with compound eyes: "For you... in one corner a slum is collapsing, in another a new train with pistons shining, in another a gaudy painted lady... You must process as one picture. What chaos! Tells you nothing, contradicts itself, changes its story. For me each tiny part has integrity, each fractionally different from the next, until all variation is accounted for, incrementally, rationally." Additionally, Lin can taste words and colours – useful for an artists without vocal chords. <br /><br />A drug called dreamshit (yes, really) is central to the story, but I doubt it will tempt experimentation: “cacophonous emotional onslaught… two or three or more moments of life were occurring at once… [he] had not come unstuck in others’ lives, but in others’ minds. He was a voyeur… These were memories. These were dreams…[He] was spattered by a psychic sluice. He felt fouled… A juddering bombardment of infinitely varied moments… he was drowning in the sloshing stuff of dreams and hopes, recollections and reflections he had never had.”<br /><br /><b>SCIENCE</b><br /><br />At times, the scientific explanations were a little long: I suspect both scientists and non-scientist would like a little less, albeit for different reasons.<br /><br />“Crisis is the energy underpinning the whole of physics. Torque’s not about physics… “it’s an entirely pathological force” that can cause mutations (transitions). A “crisis field glows by virtue of being syphoned off… perpetual fucking motion”. This too-good-to-be-true aspect reminds me somewhat of the Infinite Improbability Drive in Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy. <br /><br />Machines programmed by punchcards though, are something I can almost remember. <br /><br />The more magical thaumaturgy isn’t, thankfully, explained. <br /><br /><b>LANGUAGE</b><br /><br />Mieville loves seasoning his works with archaic words (especially those relating to the abstract and mysterious) and the odd new coinage. He also overuses a few pet words (“palimpsest” and “puissance” occur several times in everything of his I’ve read), which might annoyingly self-indulgent, but when I read so much that is breathtakingly brilliant, it's easy to overlook the odd lapse.<br /><br />In his acknowledgements, only two authors are named: Harrison (who I have yet to read) and Peake. There is certainly much of Gormenghast in characters’ names (Rudgutter, Vermishank, Maybet Slender, Magesta Barbile, Eliza Stem-Fulcher) and especially the descriptions of shadows and landscape (even though these are urban and more Dickensian). In particular, he manages to make what should be repugnant, have a sort of beauty: "Crematoria vented into the airborne ashes of wills burnt by jealous executors, which mixed with coaldust burnt to keep dying lovers warm. Thousands of sordid smoke-ghosts wrapped New Crobuzon in a stench that suffocated like guilt." Mieville's take on academia has aspects of the Cambridge colleges he knows, but also of the scholarly city of Sanctaphrax in Paul Stewart and Chris Riddell's wonderful Edge Chronicles (for ~ ages 7-11).<br /><br />I can only assume that the lack of screen versions of any of his works is because Mieville isn't keen, or is too exacting. The descriptions are so filmic, and the plots marketable, that I can imagine directors longing to do it. <br /><br />The Weaver’s style of communication provides opportunity for wonderful wordplay, and the descriptions of the slake-moths and the nightmares they invoke give Mieville full rein.<br /><br /><b>YAGHAREK and CHOICES</b><br /><br />They shy, mysterious, disgraced Yagharek is at the heart of this book – more so than Isaac and Lin. <br /><br />One of his first-person passages almost has Grimm fairy-tale elements: "I fought the barbarian prince who wanted to make a helmet out of my garuda skull and I won... Holding my intestines with one hand, I clawed his throat out with the other. I won his gold and his followers, whom I freed. I paid myself to health, bought passage on a merchant ship."<br /><br />His own kind removed his wings as a punishment for the worst sort of choice-theft (stealing from the future as well as the present), but he longs to fly again: “I was once a creature of the air, and it remembers me… it tickles me with the currents and vectors from my past… I know that something is wrong in the sky… But I am earthbound.” When he climbs high, he relishes the exhilaration, remembering his love of flight, and feeling slight memories of it.<br /><br />I was in shock when I discovered the nature of Yag's choice-theft. I’d thought of him as, to some extent, a victim (as had Isaac). Suddenly, the whole book is very obviously about choice. Should Isaac condone the crime by continuing his attempts to help Yag fly after he learns the truth? People <b>can</b> change, and they can atone for past actions by what they do afterwards. But for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[rape <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>? I want to believe in Yag, but I know that if I read a news report of a sentence being commuted because they'd been brave. selfless and transformative of society and themselves, I'd be uncomfortable, given the nature of the crime.<br /><br />I think the most interesting aspect is how Yag gains dignity by apparently shedding it. He first arrives, hiding his true self, his face and his lack of wings. Gradually, he discards his fake wooden wings, then his cloak, and finally and painfully <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[all his feathers <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. He seems to gain gravitas and confidence from each act. His final choice is to be remade in a totally different way from all the other Remades in this world (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[to pass as human, rather than garuda or Remade: “I turn away from him and step into the vastness of New Crobuzon, this towering edifice of architecture and history, this complexitude of money and slum, this profane steam-powered god. I turn and walk into the city my home, not bird or garuda, not miserable crossbreed. I turn and walk into my home, the city, a man.”  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). <br /><br />The other most significant choice relates to Andrej: in what circumstances can a non-consenting individual be sacrificed for the greater good? Surely he is a victim of choice-theft. (The question is all the more pertinent, given Isaac’s distaste for the Construct Council’s utilitarianism in similar matters and his own rejection of buying women for sex.)<br /><br /><b>THE WEAVER</b><br /><br />My first thought on encountering The Weaver was Shelob, especially the description of entering the room: "As they passed into the room, all felt a moment of dislocation, a wispy unease that prickled across their skin with a quasi-physical momentum... invisible filaments of spun aether and emotion, were draped in intricate patterns... and were rippling and sticking to the intruders."<br /><br />Its speech is described as "dream-poetics" (related to dreamshit?) and despite being in all caps, has a hypnotic other-worldliness to it: all the words are familiar (no puissant palimpsests), and yet the meaning is tantalisingly elusive. Talking to it “was like a dialog with the sleeping or the mad. It was difficult, exhausting. But it could be done.”<br />“AND THEE AND ME CONCURRED THE FAT FUNNEL-SPACE THE CLOT AT CITYWEB CENTRE SEES US CONFLAB.<br />“I TIRE AND GROW OLD AND COLD GRIMY LITTLING… THIS SIPHONING OF PHANTASMS FROM MY SOLE SOUL LEAVES ME MELANCHOLIC SEE PATTERNS INHERE EVEN IN THESE THE VORACIOUS ONES PERHAPS I JUDGE QUICK AND SLIK TASTES FALTER AND ALTER AND I AM UNSURE”<br /><br />I also like the disconnect of a dangerously amoral being, motivated purely by beauty: in this world, beauty certainly doesn't equate to goodness. <br /><br />Intriguingly, it’s the spider rather than the computer than controls the worldweb: "every possible thing ever is woven into that limitless, sprawling web. It is without beginning or end. It is complex to a degree that humbles the mind... The web is not without flaw... the worldweb flexed under the weight of time."<br /><br /><b>SAD IRONIES, MINOR ANNOYANCES AND OTHER SPOILERS</b><br /><br />These are mainly for my future reference:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Art is risky: at one point, it’s Lin’s art that saves her (Motley keeps Lin alive so she can finish her portrait of him), but it’s also her undoing (she can’t resist looking at the beautiful slake-moths).<br /><br />Isaac and his friends save the city – and yet their lives are still at risk from the militia, the Construct Council, and Motley’s gang. <br /><br />How come cactus people and khepri have nipples?!<br /><br />Some of the fight sequences were rather clichéd and hence predictable. Near the end, the militia approaching, with minutes to save the city, the deus ex of Jack-Half-a-Prayer, killing all but ONE of moths... And yet, I'm not sure how else he could have written it. Also, the militia storming the warehouse was rather slapstick. It might work better on the screen of a game or film. The first appearance of The Weaver was predictable and an easy get-out.<br /><br />I actually preferred the leisurely descriptive sections to these more action-packed ones. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>QUOTES</b><br /><br />•	First impressions of the city: “Over the engine’s oily rumble and the caresses of the river… Timbers whisper and the wind strokes thatch… Sewers riddling the earth like secular sepulchres… The rotting buildings lean against each other, exhausted… Fat predatory shadows prowl the sky.”<br />•	“The water had unpredictable qualities” Some have been known to “step into some discoloured patch of mud and start speaking long-dead languages, or find locusts in their hair, or fade slowly to translucency and disappear.”<br />•	“Light seemed to give up the struggle halfway through the thick, solid windows.”<br />•	“The cross-bred architecture of that outlandish quarter confused her: a syncresis of industrialism and the gaudy domestic ostentation of the slightly rich.”<br />•	“New Crobuzon was a city unconvinced by gravity. Aerostats oozed from clout to cloud above it like slugs on cabbages.”<br />•	“The nodes and cells of brick and wood and palsied concrete had gone rogue, spreading like malignant tumours.”<br />•	“In the outlines of stillborn streets shacks of concrete and corrugated iron blistered overnight. Inhabitation spread like mould.”<br />•	“A sickly smile grew and died on his mouth like fungus.”<br />•	“Its mutating form bubbled and welled up into strange dimensional rifts… It was alive, and then there was a time between forms when it was neither alive nor dead, but saturated with power.” After emerging from its chrysalis, “It discovered itself. It learnt its shape. It learnt it had needs.”<br />•	“Sunset bled into the canals… They ran thick and gory with light… The sky above the city was smeared with cloud.”<br />•	“It was policing by decentralised fear.”<br />•	“Patterns that rolled with hypnogogic haste… pulsing in weird dimensions.”<br />•	“The summer stretched out the daylight as if on a rack. Each moment was drawn out until its anatomy collapsed. Time broke down. The day progressed in an endless sequence of dead moments.”<br />•	“A new landscape of ruin and refuse and industrial filth was created in a speeded-up parody of the geological process.”<br />•	“scampered at obscure angles to reality.”<br />•	“Behind a beautiful palimpsest of coloured gossamer, a vast, timeless, infinite mass of absence.”<br />•	“The summer stretched out the daylight as if on a rack. Each moment was drawn out until its anatomy collapsed. Time broke down. The day progressed in an endless sequence of dead moments.”<br />•	“The psychic plane was thick with the glutinous effluvia of incomprehensible minds.”<br />•	“Shadows fell on them like predators as the light went out.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11316454055771436825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151897056?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2012">read-2012</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151897056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11193930577432822843"><b>I Love You, I Love You, I Love You</b><br/><br/>For the fortnight it took me to read this novel, I was in another world and I was in love.<br/><br/>Perhaps, now, I’ll retreat from that world and substitute another or others (or perhaps even return to my own world), but I will remain in love.<br/><br/>Is this a fantasy love or is it real? I think it’s real.<br/><br/>After all, is there any love that is not partly a product of your own mind?<br/><br/>How can a writer make this happen? How can a reader experience this? How can a person experience it i</span>
  <span id="freeText11193930577432822843" style="display:none"><b>I Love You, I Love You, I Love You</b><br /><br />For the fortnight it took me to read this novel, I was in another world and I was in love.<br /><br />Perhaps, now, I’ll retreat from that world and substitute another or others (or perhaps even return to my own world), but I will remain in love.<br /><br />Is this a fantasy love or is it real? I think it’s real.<br /><br />After all, is there any love that is not partly a product of your own mind?<br /><br />How can a writer make this happen? How can a reader experience this? How can a person experience it in real life?<br /><br /><b>SPOILER WARNING</b><br /><br />This is a sensitive review of some of the major themes of the novel. I have limited discussion of the plot, except to the extent necessary to discuss themes. I identify the antagonists, but not the process or outcome of the antagonism. Some readers feel there are spoilers.<br /><br /><b>Is This the Real Life? Is This Just Fantasy?</b><br /><br />I read a lot of fantasy and science fiction in my teens, when I was working out my taste in literature.<br /><br />I had a fairly active fantasy life. Things happened in my family life that I wanted to escape, not by imagining that they didn’t happen, but by constructing my own world, my own reality, as a refuge, as a place where I could restore myself and heal.<br /><br />This world exercised my brain like a muscle, I exerted my mind, I did not fear difficulty or effort or rationality or emotion or imagination. Instead, these things were concrete materials that I used to self-construct the me that is I.<br /><br />They allowed me to dream, to aspire, to do and to succeed. And to relax and to manage stress.<br /><br /><b>Maps and Legends</b><br /><br />Computerised war games were not an option in my childhood. I had plastic soldiers, metal tanks and artillery, model airplanes complete with World War I flying aces. Our home was raised about 1.5 meters off the ground and I built fictitious worlds in the hard black dirt, hills and valleys carved out of the soil and compacted, islands, isthmuses and peninsulas in imaginary oceans.<br /><br />Battles raged on the ground and in the air. Cannons shot matches across the rugged terrain and took the lives of the infantry. Pilots in planes suspended from the bottom of the floorboards engaged in fierce dogfights, until one or other plunged into a hillside or the watery stillness of my imaginary ocean.<br /><br />I mapped these worlds in the days before Google Earth. I created atlases of warfare, I assembled maps and legends, I taught myself German Text, so I could fill each page with my dreams. I imagined “libraries fat with forgotten volumes”, and mine.<br /><br />I wanted to be an engineer, then a cartographer, finally a diplomat. I wanted to be a lover of the foreign and the exotic, a traveller, a go-between, a communicator, an advocate, a negotiator, a master of the opening gambit, a player of trump cards, an unraveller of puzzles, a solver of problems, a snorter of diplomatic corps quality cocaine, a smoker of peace pipes, someone who might be remembered for bringing two disparate peoples (or people) together. <br /><br />Ultimately, I did not need fantasy literature, because I was my own fantasy and I created my own fantasy world. I drew my own map and I was my own legend.<br /><br />Fantasy fiction and science fiction moved on without me, while I discovered literature that helped me understand people, relationships, the world we live in.<br /><br />The closest I have got to fantasy recently has been the works of Haruki Murakami. I think I’m passionate about his novels, because they deal with the real world, almost by using fantasy as a way of seeing, a method of perception, an instrument to detect, dissect and comprehend.<br /><br />Then, China Mieville appeared on the horizon and rocketed into the relative security of my world like a V2 from “Gravity’s Rainbow”.<br /><br />“Perdido Street Station” (“PSS”) sat on my shelf unread for a few years, even after I read and enjoyed “The City and the City”.<br /><br />When Traveller started a Discussion group on CM’s works, I decided that now was the time to bite the bullet and find out what CM was really about.<br /><br />I realised, then , that I was falling in love.<br /><br /><b>”What Trick of Topography Is This?</b><br /><br />What did CM do to make me fall so?<br /><br />Let me try to tell you as best I can…<br /><br />PSS is first and foremost the tale of a city, of New Crobuzon. (I still have to pronounce this word carefully. I have never spoken it to a person in real life. Even now I mouth it hesitantly.)<br /><br />It sits astride the confluence of two rivers, the Tar and the Canker, which join to form one, the waters from the two retained, but somehow transformed, almost dialectically, into the River Gross Tar. <br /><br />New Crobuzon is an Industrial Age City-State Republic. It teems with people, beings, trade, commerce, ideas, ambitions, success, failure, suffering and misery.<br /><br />It teeters at some tipping point where just a nudge could bring the entire structure collapsing down like Einsturzende Altbauten.<br /><br />And, surely, some peril does emerge to give it that nudge, but cometh the time, cometh the man.<br /><br />Only in CM’s hands, it is not just cometh the man, it is cometh the man and the woman (women actually).<br /><br />They are not your common or garden variety heroes (just as the antagonists are not common or garden variety moths).<br /><br />They are an indie “band a parte”, in a Godardian or Tarantino-esque sense. You could almost call them a “motley crew”, except that CM has reserved that name for one of the principal antagonists.<br /><br />They are a ragamuffin bunch of outcasts with outré taste (1).<br /><br /><i>(1) Outre: <br />(a) Out of the common course or limits; extravagant; <br />(b} Bizarre; outlandish (as in, an outré costume)<br />Example:<br />My first mental development had in it much of the uncommon - even much of the outré.<br />- Edgar Allan Poe</i>.<br /><br /><b>Boys and Girls Own Adventure</b><br /><br />Even as I write this, I realise that PSS contains something of Charles Dickens, British Imperial adventure stories (especially tales of Biggles and his various sorties), pulp fiction, B-movies, French originals, Hollywood re-makes and cheap Italian rip-offs.<br /><br />To quote critic Don D'Ammassa from the Introduction to the “Encyclopedia of Adventure Fiction” (thank you Wiki):<br /><br /><i>”An adventure is an event or series of events that happens outside the course of the protagonist's ordinary life, usually accompanied by danger, often by physical action. <br /><br />“Adventure stories almost always move quickly, and the pace of the plot is at least as important as characterization, setting and other elements of a creative work.”</i><br /><br />D'Ammassa argues that the element of danger must be the principal <b>focus</b> of an adventure novel.<br /><br />For him, Charles Dickens' novel “A Tale of Two Cities” is an adventure novel because the protagonists are in constant danger of being imprisoned or killed.<br /><br />In contrast, “Great Expectations” is not, because "Pip's encounter with the convict is an adventure, but that scene is only a device to advance the main plot, which is not truly an adventure."<br /><br />While this distinction might be intellectually astute, I don’t really care to apply it to PSS.<br /><br />It is a stew with meat and vegetables. There would be no gain from an argument about what is principal or secondary.<br /><br />Suffice it to say that, what embarks from the intellectual platform later becomes a rollercoaster ride.<br /><br /><b>A Fine, but Medieval, Romance</b> <br /><br />Apart from the scientific and philosophical content, the other aspect of PSS that immediately appealed to me was the romance, especially the extent to which it was overtly erotic.<br /><br />Again, while trying to define the role of adventure in PSS, I stumbled across a discussion in Wiki, which helped me define my appreciation of the romance at the heart of the novel:<br /><br /><i>” Adventure has been a common theme since the earliest days of written fiction. Indeed, the standard plot of medieval romances was a series of adventures. <br /><br />“Following a plot framework as old as Heliodorus, and so durable as to be still alive in Hollywood movies, a hero would undergo a first set of adventures before he met his lady. A separation would follow, with a second set of adventures leading to a final reunion.”</i><br /><br />That isn’t a bad summary of the plot of PSS at an abstract level.<br /><br />At the level of a medieval romance, there is a sense in which a knight errant (Isaac), portrayed as having heroic qualities (hmm), goes on a quest and experiences various marvel-filled adventures (hmm).<br /><br />Later, these types of stories were recast from an ironic, satiric or burlesque point of view (e.g., “Don Quixote”).<br /><br />It might be more accurate to place PSS within this later romantic tradition.<br /><br />Isaac is not exactly a hero, but he is not quite an anti-hero. He is a post-modern, fat bastard, nerdy, scientific Everyman, who rises to the occasion when he is called upon.<br /><br />There is a debate in the Discussion Group as to whether he is naïve, especially because he has eschewed a traditional academic career in order to pursue his more outré personal scientific vision.<br /><br />I wouldn’t call this naïve, though I would settle for idealistic, even if it requires him to get down, get his hands dirty and even get his clothes a little grubby.<br /><br /><b>Strange Partners: The Curious Dance of the New Woman and the Imperial Adventurer</b><br /><br />I’ve borrowed this title from the name of a review by Teresa Mangum of the study, “New Woman and Colonial Adventure Fiction in Victorian Britain: Gender, Genre, Empire” by Lee Anne M. Richardson.<br /><br />If you’ll allow me to treat Isaac as an Imperial Adventurer, then Lin is the New Woman who enjoys a curious dance with him.<br /><br />It’s not just curious because of their moves, but because of their physical make-up.<br /><br />New Crobuzon doesn’t just consist of human forms, it consists of multiple sentient life forms, many of which have characteristics associated with ancient religions and mythology.<br /><br />Lin is a Khepri with a female body and a scarab beetle head.<br /><br />She is an artist with an outré taste and sensibility, while Isaac is the outcast scientist who falls in love with her, even though this is perceived as a socially taboo transgression.<br /><br />Needless to say, there is not just love, but a smorgasbord of sensory pleasures including sight, scent, taste, touch, lust and pleasure sex, notwithstanding the partial physiological incompatibility.<br /><br />Some readers seem to be grossed out by this physical challenge. I felt it highlighted the authenticity of their love. I found the relationship convincing and highly erotically charged. <br /><br />While I’m not widely read in the field of erotica and pornography, CM’s vision of this relationship really turned me on.<br /><br />It would take a great writer to write scenes of affection and delight that appealed to me as much as those featuring Lin and Isaac.<br /><br /><b>Mothrotica</b> <br /><br />Having said that, CM replicates the erotic charge in his description of the principal antagonists in the novel.<br /><br />The threat to New Crobuzon comes from giant slake-moths that feed on dreams.<br /><br />We see them transform through their life cycle from larva to pupa to adulthood, from caterpillar to cocoon to moth.<br /><br />During pupation, the larval structures of the moth are broken down, while the adult structures are formed. <br /><br />The pupa is inactive and usually static and sessile (unable to move about).<br /><br />They derive sustenance from the psychic energy of the non-rational part of the mind of those around them. They suck out the dreams of the inhabitants of New Crobuzon and leave them mindless and mute.<br /><br />Feeding is described in almost sexual terms, but having fed, the slake-moths become sexually active and fertile, they fly around, looping, falling, stroking, touching, arousing, copulating…juicily, ardently, ecstatically.<br /><br />This is gross, even though CM uses the same language he would use for Lin and Isaac.<br /><br />It’s worse when you know that slake-moths are “efficient, brilliant predators” and that they have no natural predators of their own.<br /><br />However, the juxtaposition of their non-sentient sexuality with that of Lin and Isaac makes me wonder why their sexual activity cannot be enjoyed, theoretically at least, to the same extent as other erotic behavior.<br /><br />The answer must be that it is just too remote from what we are used to, besides it might be the sentience that creates the proximity to human sexual response.<br /><br />As I’ve mentioned, even scarab beetle heads are too remote for some readers. There is a point at which sexual experience is too animal, and not sufficiently close to human sexuality to enjoy.<br /><br />However, CM at least asks us to contemplate sexuality, transgression and pleasure beyond what we are comfortable with.<br /><br /><b>Rape as Social Transgression</b> <br /><br />I won’t mention their name out of a concern for spoilers, but one of the characters is suffering a punishment for a rape.<br /><br />While this act is offensive and illegal within our society, in the context in which it occurred, it is not punishable as a crime in its own right, but as an example of depriving a person of their right to choose.<br /><br />Thus, instead of rape being cast as an offence against the body, it is cast as an offence against the mind.<br /><br />It denies the freedom of the “victim” and limits their choice.<br /><br />The penalty might be the same or even higher. However, CM uses this conceptualization of the crime to test Isaac’s reaction to the punishment.<br /><br />Initially, he sympathises with the perpetrator and can’t understand the gravity of the crime.<br /><br />Then, when he translates the act from the apparently innocuous restriction of choice to physical rape, he starts to sympathise with the “victim”, Kar’uchai, who doesn’t want to be painted as a victim and simply asks Isaac to respect the laws of her society and the punishment it regards as appropriate.<br /><br />Kar’uchai counsels Isaac not to translate her society’s laws into his social context, but instead to appreciate them on their own terms.<br /><br />Ultimately, despite his affinity with his friend, Isaac elects to respect the law of her society, to respect its sovereignty, almost as if it was primarily an issue of international relations.<br /><br />Obviously, it is still a moral and criminal issue. However, Isaac accepts that each society has the right to define and enforce its own practices, customs and laws.<br /><br />It is not always appropriate, at least in the quasi-Victorian Steampunk era, to judge a society from the outside.<br /><br /><b>Isaac’s “Principia Mothematica”</b><br /><br />Isaac has had a number of scientific interests during his career, including Unified Field Theory, where his allegiance is to Moving Theory rather than Static Theory.<br /><br />However, the interest most relevant to the battle with the slake-moths is crisis energy and his invention of a crisis engine.<br /><br />So what is crisis energy?<br /><br />This is major shit we’re dealing with here, mate, so…um…pay attention.<br /><br />1. We’re talking about the energy in matter such as an object.<br /><br />2. There are three types of energy: kinetic, potential and crisis.<br /><br />3. Kinetic energy is the energy the object possesses due to its motion.<br /><br />4. Potential energy is the energy the object has when it’s placed in a position, so that it’s teetering or about to change its state (e.g., you’ve lifted it up and are about to drop it).<br /><br />5. Crisis energy is the energy that is inside an object by virtue of its being or existence. It’s not necessarily there because it’s moving or because it’s been moved to a particular position. It’s there, because it’s already in the object, wherever it is or wherever it’s placed. There are incredible tensions within any object, no matter what its state. If it can be moved toward a state of crisis, a point when it is about to change its form or state (a transformation or transmogrification), the crisis energy will manifest itself, and can be tapped.<br /><br />Isaac’s goal is to tap or channel or harness crisis energy. If he succeeds, he will virtually have achieved a perpetual energy source and perpetual motion. <br /><br />He achieves his goal by constructing a crisis engine that can channel energy and amplify the output.<br /><br />Whereas Thomas Pynchon’s “Gravity’s Rainbow” is about entropy, PSS is about the exact opposite: the maximization of energy.<br /><br />The rest is secret. (I shouldn’t even have told you what’s above. I’m going to get into trouble with the Moderator General.)<br /><br /><b>Ethical Cleansing</b> <br /><br />Ultimately, it’s up to Isaac and his crisis engine to wage war on the slake-moths.<br /><br />Because everybody in New Crobuzon is threatened by the moths, various coalitions are proposed.<br /><br />Isaac’s best prospect comes from the Construct Council, which is a machine that is on the fringe of acquiring an artificial intelligence called “constructed intelligence”, a mechanical form of sentience. It’s just a calculating intelligence that is trying to “self-construct” or maximize itself, to ascend a hierarchy of intelligence.<br /><br />While he partners it, ultimately, he does not trust it totally, because it has no empathy or morals:<br /><br /><i>”It'll do whatever it has to - it'll lie to us, it'll kill - to increase its power.”</i><br /><br />Whatever, he cannot let it have unrestricted access to the crisis engine. (Cue dramatic music.)<br /><br />Just as Isaac tried to take an objective ethical stance with respect to rape, he does the same with the war on the slake-moths.<br /><br /><b>The Nectar of the Subconscious</b> <br /><br />The slake-moths are dream eaters that prey on the subconscious. They detect and suck out hidden thoughts, guilty thoughts, anxieties, delights, dreams: <br /><br /><i>"They drink the peculiar brew that results from self-reflexive thought, when the instincts and needs and desires and intuitions are folded in on themselves and we reflect on our thoughts and then reflect on the reflection, endlessly... Our thoughts ferment like the purest liquor...not the meat-calories slopping about in the brainpan, but the fine wine of sapience and sentience itself, the subconscious. Dreams."</i><br /><br />They leave their victims mindless and imbecile.<br /><br />While calculating or thinking machines might be able to replicate rationality, they cannot replicate morality. They can replicate consciousness, but they cannot replicate a conscience:<br /><br /><i>” I do not dream. I am a calculating machine that has calculated how to think. I do not dream. I have no neuroses, no hidden depths. My consciousness is a growing function of my processing power, not the baroque thing that sprouts from your mind, with its hidden rooms in attics and cellars."”</i><br /><br />CM’s message seems to be that rationality is not enough to make us human. We need our passions and morality as well. We need our sentiments to be sentient. We need experience to be sapient.<br /><br />Isaac, the fat bastard outcast scientist has mastered reason, but it is not enough. He has stared the Enlightenment in the face and realized there is More Than This.<br /><br />Reason will allow us to peak through the keyhole, but if we want to open the doors of perception, we need more.<br /><br /><b>The Transformation of Love</b><br /><br />Much of PSS is concerned with transformation, transgression and translation, the movement from one state to another.<br /><br />Lin is caught in a “bastard zone”, Motley is trapped in a “ruptured moment”.<br /><br />Perhaps, love is the drug, the remedy that is required.<br /><br />The romantic in me wants to believe that CM regards love as a raptured moment that will stand in opposition to or on the shoulders of pure reason.<br /><br />The relationship between Lin and Isaac is a clue to the transformation he thinks might be required.<br /><br />Their love, transgressive as it might be, consists of “filthy and loving invitations” as well as “jokes and apologies and compliments and lust.”<br /><br />Lin longs “to come home every night to freshly mixed fruit salad and theatre tickets and sex."<br /><br />Isaac would be content just to look into the eyes of his scarab queen and hear her ask, “How was it, treasure?”<br /><br />Some time during the night, they might even be heard to cry out, “I Love You, I Love You, I Love You.”<br /><br />It’s up to each of us to decide whether this is a love worth having.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11193930577432822843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345443021" href="/book/show/867383.Perdido_Street_Station">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer431992826" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6531811920425223345">I'm not feeling overly inspired to review this book. I was. At first. At around the 300 page mark, still riveted by the world that Miéville created, I started feverishly composing what would have been... what could have been... <br/><br/>I researched Miéville's background and was prepared to tell you all about his growing up in a lower-class household with just his mum and his sister, but that he was super smart and won scholarships to all the best schools. I was going to tell you about his love for role-</span>
  <span id="freeText6531811920425223345" style="display:none">I'm not feeling overly inspired to review this book. I was. At first. At around the 300 page mark, still riveted by the world that Miéville created, I started feverishly composing what would have been... what could have been... <br><br>I researched Miéville's background and was prepared to tell you all about his growing up in a lower-class household with just his mum and his sister, but that he was super smart and won scholarships to all the best schools. I was going to tell you about his love for role-playing games and how his writing was particularly influenced by Dungeons and Dragons, and I was going to tell you how he wrote this novel while working on his PhD. in Philosophy of International Law. But then, I don't know, I guess I got bored... <br><br>I was really keen to talk about all of the cool mythological creatures that Miéville referenced to create the world of Bas-Lag.<br><br>Like the vodyanoi -- these neat-o creatures found in Slavic mythology, "Vodyanoy is said to appear as a naked old man with a greenish beard and long hair, with his body covered in algae and muck, usually covered in black fish scales. He has webbed paws instead of hands, a fish's tail, eyes that burn like red-hot coals. He usually rides along his river on a half-sunk log, making loud splashes." (Wikipedia)<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380370239i/723058.jpg" width="220" height="286" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And the khepri, a god of ancient Egyptian religions with the body of man and head of a scarab<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393975i/758559.png" width="220" height="471" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But I guess I got lazy...<br><br>I was going to compose a list of the new words I learned, but it got too long...<br><br>I was going to talk about how the setting for the book was influenced by The Malacia Tapestry &amp; Anubis Gates. I figured I could tell you a bit about each and in what ways New Crobuzon relates, but frankly I just don't care anymore...<br><br>The book went on for too long. I lost my groove. I still enjoyed it, but by the end, I didn't care what was happening anymore, I just wanted it to end. And no amount of staring at China's hot photo on the back cover with those pouty lips could change that. <br><br>Funny, back when I was researching what would have been an EPIC review, I found an interview where China talks about his own tendency to overwrite. Here's an excerpt: "If you look at the way critics describe Lovecraft, for example, they often say he’s purple, overwritten, overblown, verbose, but it’s unputdownable. There’s something about that kind of hallucinatorily intense purple prose which completely breaches all rules of “good writing,” but is somehow utterly compulsive and affecting. That pulp aesthetic of language is something very tenuous, which all too easily simply becomes shit, but is fascinating where it works. Though I also love much more minimalist writers, it’s that lush approach that I’m drawn to in terms of my own writing, for good and bad."<br><br>For the most part, I found his lush approach really worked. For the most part. But like I said, it didn't hold my attention the whole way through. He lost me about 3/4 of the way in. I just didn't care. Maybe it's my fault as a reader. I didn't feel connected enough to the characters to care one way or the other what happened at the end. <br><br>So, here I am, looking at China's handsome face on the back cover of the book. I'm writing this lackluster review while he gives me this "are you really only going to give me three stars??" look. Alright, since you're so damn good lookin' here's an extra half star. 3.5/5 Now take your pouty handsome face and go learn how to edit. </span>

<div id="review_570801209" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570801209" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570801209?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=steampunk">steampunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=biopunk">biopunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2016-shelf">2016-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570801209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6731393331839990932">Lesson learned after reading this?<br/><br/>Don't Experiment With Cheese.<br/><br/>Can you imagine how many problems could have been avoided had this novel had access to time-travel? It's practically the only trope not explored, and that's saying a damn lot.<br/><br/>Off and on through the entire reading, I wanted to declare that this is one of the most brilliant novels ever written. The sheer level of creativity and attention to detail, the fantastic explorations of ideas, the explosion of plot items and complications, and </span>
  <span id="freeText6731393331839990932" style="display:none">Lesson learned after reading this?<br /><br />Don't Experiment With Cheese.<br /><br />Can you imagine how many problems could have been avoided had this novel had access to time-travel? It's practically the only trope not explored, and that's saying a damn lot.<br /><br />Off and on through the entire reading, I wanted to declare that this is one of the most brilliant novels ever written. The sheer level of creativity and attention to detail, the fantastic explorations of ideas, the explosion of plot items and complications, and the REALITY of it all just begged to be placed up there as one of the very, very best speculative fiction ever written.<br /><br />It's dense, but not unaccessible. The characters are vivid and fascinating and it's so easy to pick them all out of a lineup, despite there being a huge number of incidentals. And the plot is about as windy as they come while still holding to the straight and true. After things go to hell, it's practically a straight line, in fact, but that line is rich and a seemingly impossible goal.<br /><br />I never stopped being impressed by the novel, whether it was my first time reading it or this second time. <br /><br />But here's the "But".<br /><br />I can't believe I'm saying this, but there was too much action after the moths. The city is as rich as they come, and so many damn things happened, including a great invasion scene, mind-sucking beasties, aerial monster sex, Steampunk AI emergence, mind-shit, and so many, many aliens. (Or whatever you want to call them.)<br /><br />All the little things, all the attempts to put the genie back in the bottle, all the tiring attempts to right past wrongs, it was all just too draining for me. After a certain point, it was all brilliant descriptions and fascinating reveals, and by themselves I have no complaint. It was all tension and almost no release. As a thought experiment, I give it top marks in idea and execution. As a strictly enjoyable novel that lets the reader breathe every once in a while... well, not so much.<br /><br />I'd almost recommend taking a break every once in a while, except that there's so many details to juggle and appreciate that I'd be afraid that I'd lose the thread. Not that the main thread was ever difficult to pick up again, of course, because like I said, it was pretty much a straight line for almost 3/5 of the novel. I found myself wishing for more dialogue and character stuff the way we had during the opening before the Crisis Engine was first turned on. <br /><br />It was brilliant afterward, but it needed cycles and rhythm. It was frankly exhausting, even when I marvelled at how beautiful it was.<br /><br />I can make a good argument that the main character of the novel was Perdido Street Station, itself, and Isaac and Yagharek and Lin merely being secondary characters.<br /><br />It's not entirely true, of course, and I sincerely liked the flesh and blood characters right along the interesting constructs. I even like Isaac despite being the author of all this mess and his many fuck-ups. I even like Yagharek despite the shit he pulled, even if I agree with his judgement.<br /><br />It's a depressing end to the novel, too, so perhaps my ongoing excitement for the tale took a downturn along with the tone.<br /><br />Nothing it going to stop me, this time, from reading the Bas-Lag sequels. I didn't have any excuse last time, and I'm frankly chomping at the bit to read more of this world. It's so damn rich, and not even an unsatisfying end can ruin that. :) </span>
  <a data-text-id="6731393331839990932" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
